<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Lithium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 3</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. For the use of lithium as a medication, see <a href="/wiki/Lithium_(medication)" title="Lithium (medication)">Lithium (medication)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Lithium_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Lithium (disambiguation)">Lithium (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_lithium" title="Template:Infobox lithium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_lithium" title="Template talk:Infobox lithium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_lithium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Lithium</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">λίθος</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language transliteration">lithos</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>&#160;</small>&#39;stone&#39;) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Li</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;3. It is a soft, silvery-white <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a>, it is the lightest metal and the lightest solid element. Like all alkali metals, lithium is highly <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">reactive</a> and flammable, and is stored in <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oil</a>. When cut, it exhibits a metallic <a href="/wiki/Luster_(mineralogy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Luster (mineralogy)">luster</a>, but moist air <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrodes</a> it quickly to a dull silvery gray, then black tarnish. It never occurs freely in nature, but only in (usually ionic) <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Pegmatite" title="Pegmatite">pegmatitic</a> minerals which were once the main source of lithium. Due to its solubility as an ion, it is present in ocean water and is commonly obtained from <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brines</a>. Lithium metal is isolated <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolytically</a> from a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">lithium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of the lithium atom verges on instability, since the two stable lithium <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> found in nature have among the lowest <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">binding energies</a> per <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> of all stable <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a>. Because of its relative nuclear instability, lithium is less common in the solar system than 25 of the first 32 chemical elements even though its nuclei are very light: it is an exception to the trend that heavier nuclei are less common.<sup id="cite_ref-Lodders2003_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders2003-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> For related reasons, lithium has important uses in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear physics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> of lithium atoms to <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> in 1932 was the first fully man-made <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_deuteride" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium deuteride">lithium deuteride</a> serves as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> fuel in <a href="/wiki/Teller-Ulam_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Teller-Ulam design">staged thermonuclear weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium and its compounds have several industrial applications, including heat-resistant glass and <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_grease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium grease">lithium grease</a> lubricants, flux additives for iron, steel and aluminium production, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_battery" title="Lithium battery">lithium batteries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion batteries</a>. These uses consume more than three quarters of lithium production.
</p><p>Lithium is present in biological systems in trace amounts; its functions are uncertain. Lithium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> have proven to be useful as a <a href="/wiki/Mood_stabilizer" title="Mood stabilizer">mood-stabilizing</a> drug in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a> in humans.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_and_physical">Atomic and physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Atomic and physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Limetal.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Limetal.JPG/150px-Limetal.JPG" width="150" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Limetal.JPG/225px-Limetal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Limetal.JPG/300px-Limetal.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Limetal.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lithium ingots with a thin layer of black nitride tarnish</div></div></div>
<p>Like the other <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a>, lithium has a single <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electron</a> that is easily given up to form a <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Because of this, lithium is a good conductor of heat and electricity as well as a highly reactive element, though it is the least reactive of the alkali metals. Lithium's low reactivity is due to the proximity of its valence electron to its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> (the remaining <a href="/wiki/Two-electron_atom" title="Two-electron atom">two electrons</a> are in the <a href="/wiki/S-orbital" class="mw-redirect" title="S-orbital">1s orbital</a>, much lower in energy, and do not participate in chemical bonds).<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium metal is soft enough to be cut with a knife. When cut, it possesses a silvery-white color that quickly changes to gray as it oxidizes to <a href="/wiki/Lithium_oxide" title="Lithium oxide">lithium oxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> While it has one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_elements_by_melting_point" class="mw-redirect" title="List of elements by melting point">lowest melting points</a> among all metals (180&#160;°C), it has the highest melting and boiling points of the alkali metals.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium has a very low density (0.534&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>), comparable with pine wood. It is the least dense of all elements that are solids at room temperature; the next lightest solid element (potassium, at 0.862&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>) is more than 60% denser. Furthermore, apart from <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, it is less dense than any liquid element, being only two thirds as dense as <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a> (0.808&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Lithium can float on the lightest hydrocarbon oils and is one of only three metals that can float on water, the other two being <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lithium_element.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lithium_element.jpg/150px-Lithium_element.jpg" width="150" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lithium_element.jpg/225px-Lithium_element.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lithium_element.jpg/300px-Lithium_element.jpg 2x" data-file-width="345" data-file-height="511" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lithium_element.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lithium floating in oil</div></div></div>
<p>Lithium's <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion">coefficient of thermal expansion</a> is twice that of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and almost four times that of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Lithium is <a href="/wiki/Superconductive" class="mw-redirect" title="Superconductive">superconductive</a> below 400 <a href="/wiki/Microkelvin" class="mw-redirect" title="Microkelvin">μK</a> at standard pressure<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and at higher temperatures (more than 9 K) at very high pressures (&gt;20 GPa).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> At temperatures below 70 K, lithium, like sodium, undergoes <a href="/wiki/Diffusionless_transformations" class="mw-redirect" title="Diffusionless transformations">diffusionless phase change transformations</a>. At 4.2 K it has a <a href="/wiki/Rhombohedral_crystal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhombohedral crystal system">rhombohedral crystal system</a> (with a nine-layer repeat spacing); at higher temperatures it transforms to <a href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a>. At liquid-helium temperatures (4 K) the rhombohedral structure is prevalent.<sup id="cite_ref-overhauser_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-overhauser-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Multiple allotropic forms have been identified for lithium at high pressures.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium has a mass <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a>  of 3.58 kilojoules per kilogram-kelvin, the highest of all solids.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Because of this, lithium metal is often used in <a href="/wiki/Coolant" title="Coolant">coolants</a> for <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat transfer</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemistry and compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium reacts with water easily, but with noticeably less vigor than other alkali metals. The reaction forms <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">lithium hydroxide</a> in aqueous solution.<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Because of its reactivity with water, lithium is usually stored in a hydrocarbon sealant, often <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_jelly" title="Petroleum jelly">petroleum jelly</a>. Though the heavier alkali metals can be stored in more dense substances, such as <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oil</a>, lithium is not dense enough to be fully submerged in these liquids.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In moist air, lithium rapidly tarnishes to form a black coating of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">lithium hydroxide</a> (LiOH and LiOH·H<sub>2</sub>O), <a href="/wiki/Lithium_nitride" title="Lithium nitride">lithium nitride</a> (Li<sub>3</sub>N) and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">lithium carbonate</a> (Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, the result of a secondary reaction between LiOH and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a>).<sup id="cite_ref-kamienski_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kamienski-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png/220px-Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png/330px-Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png/440px-Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png 2x" data-file-width="1088" data-file-height="1100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Butyllithium-hexamer-from-xtal-3D-balls-A.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hexameric structure of the <a href="/wiki/N-butyllithium" class="mw-redirect" title="N-butyllithium">n-butyllithium</a> fragment in a crystal</div></div></div>
<p>When placed over a flame, lithium compounds give off a striking crimson color, but when it burns strongly the flame becomes a brilliant silver. Lithium will ignite and burn in oxygen when exposed to water or water vapors.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Lithium is <a href="/wiki/Flammable" class="mw-redirect" title="Flammable">flammable</a>, and it is potentially explosive when exposed to air and especially to water, though less so than the other <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a>. The lithium-water reaction at normal temperatures is brisk but nonviolent because the hydrogen produced does not ignite on its own. As with all alkali metals, lithium fires are difficult to extinguish, requiring dry powder fire extinguishers (<a href="/wiki/Class_D_fire_extinguisher" class="mw-redirect" title="Class D fire extinguisher">Class D</a> type). Lithium is one of the few metals that react with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">normal conditions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium has a <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_relationship" title="Diagonal relationship">diagonal relationship</a> with <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, an element of similar atomic and <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">ionic radius</a>. Chemical resemblances between the two metals include the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">nitride</a> by reaction with N<sub>2</sub>, the formation of an <a href="/wiki/Lithium_oxide" title="Lithium oxide">oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) and peroxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) when burnt in O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> with similar <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubilities</a>, and thermal instability of the <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> and nitrides.<sup id="cite_ref-kamienski_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kamienski-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The metal reacts with hydrogen gas at high temperatures to produce <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">lithium hydride</a> (LiH).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other known <a href="/wiki/Binary_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary compound">binary compounds</a> include <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_fluoride" title="Lithium fluoride">LiF</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">LiCl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_bromide" title="Lithium bromide">LiBr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iodide" title="Lithium iodide">LiI</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_sulfide" title="Lithium sulfide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span></a>), <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_superoxide" title="Lithium superoxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">LiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>), and <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbide" title="Lithium carbide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>). Many other inorganic compounds are known in which lithium combines with <a href="/wiki/Anion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anion">anions</a> to form salts: <a href="/wiki/Lithium_borate" title="Lithium borate">borates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_amide" title="Lithium amide">amides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">carbonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_nitrate" title="Lithium nitrate">nitrate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Borohydride" title="Borohydride">borohydride</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_borohydride" title="Lithium borohydride"><span class="chemf nowrap">LiBH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>). <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">Lithium aluminium hydride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">LiAlH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) is commonly used as a reducing agent in organic synthesis.
</p><p>Multiple <a href="/wiki/Organolithium_reagent" title="Organolithium reagent">organolithium reagents</a> are known in which there is a direct <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">bond</a> between <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and lithium atoms, effectively creating a <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanion</a>. These are extremely powerful <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">bases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nucleophile" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon nucleophile">nucleophiles</a>. In many of these organolithium compounds, the lithium ions tend to aggregate into high-symmetry clusters by themselves, which is relatively common for alkali cations.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/LiHe" title="LiHe">LiHe</a>, a very weakly interacting <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Van der Waals compound">van der Waals compound</a>, has been detected at very low temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lithium" title="Isotopes of lithium">Isotopes of lithium</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring lithium is composed of two stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <sup>6</sup>Li and <sup>7</sup>Li, the latter being the more abundant (92.5% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isotopesproject_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isotopesproject-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Both natural isotopes have anomalously low <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a> per nucleon (compared to the neighboring elements on the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_chart_of_the_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodic chart of the elements">periodic table</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>); lithium is the only low numbered element that can produce net energy through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>. The two lithium nuclei have lower binding energies per nucleon than any other stable nuclides other than <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bind_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bind-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> As a result of this, though very light in atomic weight, lithium is less common in the Solar System than 25 of the first 32 chemical elements.<sup id="cite_ref-Lodders2003_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders2003-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Seven <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, the most stable being <sup>8</sup>Li with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 838 <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">ms</a> and <sup>9</sup>Li with a half-life of 178&#160;ms. All of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are shorter than 8.6&#160;ms. The shortest-lived isotope of lithium is <sup>4</sup>Li, which decays through <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a> and has a half-life of 7.6 × 10<sup>−23</sup> s.<sup id="cite_ref-nuclidetable_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclidetable-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>7</sup>Li is one of the <a href="/wiki/Primordial_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Primordial elements">primordial elements</a> (or, more properly, primordial <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a>) produced in <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>. A small amount of both <sup>6</sup>Li and <sup>7</sup>Li are produced in stars, but are thought to be "<a href="/wiki/Lithium_burning" title="Lithium burning">burned</a>" as fast as produced.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Additional small amounts of lithium of both <sup>6</sup>Li and <sup>7</sup>Li may be generated from solar wind, cosmic rays hitting heavier atoms, and from early solar system <sup>7</sup><a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">Be</a> and <sup>10</sup>Be radioactive decay.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> While lithium is created in stars during <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, it is further burned. <sup>7</sup>Li can also be generated in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_star" title="Carbon star">carbon stars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium isotopes fractionate substantially during a wide variety of natural processes,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> including mineral formation (chemical precipitation), <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a>. Lithium ions substitute for <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and iron in octahedral sites in <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> minerals, where <sup>6</sup>Li is preferred to <sup>7</sup>Li, resulting in enrichment of the light isotope in processes of hyperfiltration and rock alteration. The exotic <sup>11</sup>Li is known to exhibit a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_halo" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear halo">nuclear halo</a>. The process known as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_vapor_laser_isotope_separation" title="Atomic vapor laser isotope separation">laser isotope separation</a> can be used to separate lithium isotopes, in particular <sup>7</sup>Li from <sup>6</sup>Li.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear weapons manufacture and other nuclear physics applications are a major source of artificial lithium fractionation, with the light isotope <sup>6</sup>Li being retained by industry and military stockpiles to such an extent that it has caused slight but measurable change in the <sup>6</sup>Li to <sup>7</sup>Li ratios in natural sources, such as rivers. This has led to unusual uncertainty in the standardized <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> of lithium, since this quantity depends on the natural abundance ratios of these naturally-occurring stable lithium isotopes, as they are available in commercial lithium mineral sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Coplen2002_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coplen2002-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both stable isotopes of lithium can be <a href="/wiki/Laser_cooling" title="Laser cooling">laser cooled</a> and were used to produce the first quantum degenerate <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose–Einstein condensate">Bose</a>-<a href="/wiki/Fermionic_condensate" title="Fermionic condensate">Fermi</a> mixture.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elemental_abundances.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/350px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png" width="350" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/525px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Elemental_abundances.svg/700px-Elemental_abundances.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elemental_abundances.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lithium is about as common as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> in the Earth's upper continental <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>, on a per-atom basis.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical">Astronomical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Astronomical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">Nucleosynthesis</a></div>
<p>Though it was synthesized in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, lithium (together with beryllium and boron), is markedly less abundant in the universe than other elements. This is a result of the comparatively low stellar temperatures necessary to destroy lithium, along with a lack of common processes to produce it.<sup id="cite_ref-wesleyan_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wesleyan-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to modern cosmological theory, lithium—in both stable isotopes (lithium-6 and lithium-7)—was one of the three elements <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">synthesized</a> in the Big Bang.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Though the amount of lithium generated in <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> is dependent upon the number of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> per <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryon</a>, for accepted values the lithium abundance can be calculated, and there is a "cosmological lithium discrepancy" in the universe: older stars seem to have less lithium than they should, and some younger stars have much more.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The lack of lithium in older stars is apparently caused by the "mixing" of lithium into the interior of stars, where it is destroyed,<sup id="cite_ref-cld_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cld-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> while lithium is produced in younger stars. Though it transmutes into two atoms of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> due to collision with a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> at temperatures above 2.4 million degrees Celsius (most stars easily attain this temperature in their interiors), lithium is more abundant than current computations would predict in later-generation stars.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg/220px-Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg/330px-Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg/440px-Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nova_Centauri_2013_ESO.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nova_Centauri_2013" title="Nova Centauri 2013">Nova Centauri 2013</a> is the first in which evidence of lithium has been found.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Lithium is also found in <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> substellar objects and certain anomalous orange stars. Because lithium is present in cooler, less-massive brown dwarfs, but is destroyed in hotter <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> stars, its presence in the stars' spectra can be used in the "lithium test" to differentiate the two, as both are smaller than the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Certain orange stars can also contain a high concentration of lithium. Those orange stars found to have a higher than usual concentration of lithium (such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Centaurus_X-4&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Centaurus X-4 (page does not exist)">Centaurus X-4</a>) orbit massive objects—neutron stars or black holes—whose gravity evidently pulls heavier lithium to the surface of a hydrogen-helium star, causing more lithium to be observed.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terrestrial">Terrestrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Terrestrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Lithium_minerals" title="Category:Lithium minerals">Lithium minerals</a></div>
<p>Although lithium is widely distributed on Earth, it does not naturally occur in elemental form due to its high reactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The total lithium content of seawater is very large and is estimated as 230 billion tonnes, where the element exists at a relatively constant concentration of 0.14 to 0.25 parts per million (ppm),<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enc_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> or 25 <a href="/wiki/Micromolar" class="mw-redirect" title="Micromolar">micromolar</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> higher concentrations approaching 7 ppm are found near <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates for the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crustal</a> content range from 20 to 70 ppm by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-kamienski_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kamienski-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In keeping with its name, lithium forms a minor part of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a>, with the largest concentrations in <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granites</a>. Granitic <a href="/wiki/Pegmatite" title="Pegmatite">pegmatites</a> also provide the greatest abundance of lithium-containing minerals, with <a href="/wiki/Spodumene" title="Spodumene">spodumene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petalite" title="Petalite">petalite</a> being the most commercially viable sources.<sup id="cite_ref-kamienski_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kamienski-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Another significant mineral of lithium is <a href="/wiki/Lepidolite" title="Lepidolite">lepidolite</a> which is now an obsolete name for a series formed by polylithionite and trilithionite.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A newer source for lithium is <a href="/wiki/Hectorite" title="Hectorite">hectorite</a> clay, the only active development of which is through the Western Lithium Corporation in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> At 20&#160;mg lithium per kg of Earth's crust,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> lithium is the 25th most abundant element.
</p><p>According to the <i>Handbook of Lithium and Natural Calcium</i>, "Lithium is a comparatively rare element, although it is found in many rocks and some brines, but always in very low concentrations. There are a fairly large number of both lithium mineral and brine deposits but only comparatively few of them are of actual or potential commercial value. Many are very small, others are too low in grade."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="US Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a> estimates that in 2010, Chile had the largest reserves by far (7.5 million tonnes)<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and the highest annual production (8,800 tonnes). One of the largest <i>reserve bases</i><sup id="cite_ref-res_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-res-49">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> of lithium is in the <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Salar de Uyuni</a> area of Bolivia, which has 5.4 million tonnes. Other major suppliers include Australia, Argentina and China.<sup id="cite_ref-minerals.usgs.gov_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minerals.usgs.gov-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meridian_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meridian-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> As of 2015, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Czech_Geological_Survey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Czech Geological Survey (page does not exist)">Czech Geological Survey</a> considered the entire <a href="/wiki/Ore_Mountains" title="Ore Mountains">Ore Mountains</a> in the Czech Republic as lithium province. Five deposits are registered, one near <a href="/w/index.php?title=C%C3%ADnovec&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cínovec (page does not exist)">Cínovec</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://cs.wikipedia.org/wiki/C%C3%ADnovec_(Dub%C3%AD)" class="extiw" title="cs:Cínovec (Dubí)">cs</a>)</span>  is considered as a potentially economical deposit, with 160 000 tonnes of lithium.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2010, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that American geologists were conducting ground surveys on <a href="/wiki/Dry_lake" title="Dry lake">dry</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a> in western Afghanistan believing that large deposits of lithium are located there. "Pentagon officials said that their initial analysis at one location in <a href="/wiki/Ghazni_Province" title="Ghazni Province">Ghazni Province</a> showed the potential for lithium deposits as large as those of Bolivia, which now has the world's largest known lithium reserves."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> These estimates are "based principally on old data, which was gathered mainly by the Soviets during their occupation of Afghanistan from 1979–1989".  Stephen Peters, the head of the USGS's Afghanistan Minerals Project, said that he was unaware of USGS involvement in any new surveying for minerals in Afghanistan in the past two years. 'We are not aware of any discoveries of lithium,' he said."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lithia_water" title="Lithia water">Lithia</a> ("lithium brine") is associated with <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> mining areas in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, England and an evaluation project from 400-meter deep test boreholes is under consideration. If successful the hot brines will also provide <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a> to power the lithium extraction and refining process.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological">Biological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Biological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium is found in trace amount in numerous plants, plankton, and invertebrates, at concentrations of 69 to 5,760 parts per billion (ppb). In vertebrates the concentration is slightly lower, and nearly all vertebrate tissue and body fluids contain lithium ranging from 21 to 763 ppb.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Marine organisms tend to bioaccumulate lithium more than terrestrial organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Whether lithium has a physiological role in any of these organisms is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg/220px-Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg" width="220" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg/330px-Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg/440px-Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="461" data-file-height="509" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arfwedson_Johan_A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Johan August Arfwedson is credited with the discovery of lithium in 1817</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Petalite" title="Petalite">Petalite</a> (LiAlSi<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub>) was discovered in 1800 by the Brazilian chemist and statesman <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Bonif%C3%A1cio_de_Andrada_e_Silva" class="mw-redirect" title="José Bonifácio de Andrada e Silva">José Bonifácio de Andrada e Silva</a> in a mine on the island of <a href="/wiki/Ut%C3%B6,_Sweden" title="Utö, Sweden">Utö, Sweden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mindat_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mindat-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-discovery_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> However, it was not until 1817 that <a href="/wiki/Johan_August_Arfwedson" title="Johan August Arfwedson">Johan August Arfwedson</a>, then working in the laboratory of the chemist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" class="mw-redirect" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">detected</a> the presence of a new element while analyzing petalite ore.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-berzelius_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berzelius-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uwis_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uwis-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vanderkrogt_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanderkrogt-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> This element formed compounds similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, though its <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">carbonate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">hydroxide</a> were less <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble in water</a> and more <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">alkaline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-compounds_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compounds-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Berzelius gave the alkaline material the name "<i>lithion</i>/<i>lithina</i>", from the Greek word <i>λιθoς</i> (transliterated as <i>lithos</i>, meaning "stone"), to reflect its discovery in a solid mineral, as opposed to potassium, which had been discovered in plant ashes, and sodium, which was known partly for its high abundance in animal blood. He named the metal inside the material "lithium".<sup id="cite_ref-krebs_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krebs-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vanderkrogt_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanderkrogt-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Arfwedson later showed that this same element was present in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Spodumene" title="Spodumene">spodumene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepidolite" title="Lepidolite">lepidolite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1818, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gmelin" title="Christian Gmelin">Christian Gmelin</a> was the first to observe that lithium salts give a bright red color to flame.<sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> However, both Arfwedson and Gmelin tried and failed to isolate the pure element from its salts.<sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vanderkrogt_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanderkrogt-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eote_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eote-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It was not isolated until 1821, when <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_Brande" title="William Thomas Brande">William Thomas Brande</a> obtained it by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_oxide" title="Lithium oxide">lithium oxide</a>, a process that had previously been employed by the chemist Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> to isolate the alkali metals potassium and sodium.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eote_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eote-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Brande also described some pure salts of lithium, such as the chloride, and, estimating that lithia (<a href="/wiki/Lithium_oxide" title="Lithium oxide">lithium oxide</a>) contained about 55% metal, estimated the atomic weight of lithium to be around 9.8 g/mol (modern value ~6.94 g/mol).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In 1855, larger quantities of lithium were produced through the electrolysis of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">lithium chloride</a> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bunsen" title="Robert Bunsen">Robert Bunsen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Matthiessen" title="Augustus Matthiessen">Augustus Matthiessen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The discovery of this procedure led to commercial production of lithium in 1923 by the German company <a href="/wiki/Metallgesellschaft_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Metallgesellschaft AG">Metallgesellschaft AG</a>, which performed an electrolysis of a liquid mixture of lithium chloride and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-webelementshistory_59-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webelementshistory-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The production and use of lithium underwent several drastic changes in history. The first major application of lithium was in high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Lithium_grease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium grease">lithium greases</a> for aircraft engines and similar applications in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and shortly after. This use was supported by the fact that lithium-based soaps have a higher melting point than other alkali soaps, and are less corrosive than calcium based soaps. The small demand for lithium soaps and lubricating greases was supported by several small mining operations, mostly in the US.
</p><p>The demand for lithium increased dramatically during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> with the production of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_design" title="Nuclear weapon design">nuclear fusion weapons</a>. Both lithium-6 and lithium-7 produce <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> when irradiated by neutrons, and are thus useful for the production of tritium by itself, as well as a form of solid fusion fuel used inside hydrogen bombs in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_deuteride" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium deuteride">lithium deuteride</a>. The US became the prime producer of lithium between the late 1950s and the mid 1980s. At the end, the stockpile of lithium was roughly 42,000 tonnes of lithium hydroxide. The stockpiled lithium was depleted in lithium-6 by 75%, which was enough to affect the measured <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> of lithium in many standardized chemicals, and even the atomic weight of lithium in some "natural sources" of lithium ion which had been "contaminated" by lithium salts discharged from isotope separation facilities, which had found its way into ground water.<sup id="cite_ref-Coplen2002_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coplen2002-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USGSCR1994_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCR1994-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Lithium was used to decrease the melting temperature of glass and to improve the melting behavior of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> when using the <a href="/wiki/Hall-H%C3%A9roult_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Hall-Héroult process">Hall-Héroult process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DeberitzBoche2003_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeberitzBoche2003-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bauer1985_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer1985-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> These two uses dominated the market until the middle of the 1990s. After the end of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">nuclear arms race</a>, the demand for lithium decreased and the sale of department of energy stockpiles on the open market further reduced prices.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCR1994_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCR1994-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In the mid 1990s, several companies started to extract lithium from <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> which proved to be a less expensive option than underground or open-pit mining. Most of the mines closed or shifted their focus to other materials because only the ore from zoned pegmatites could be mined for a competitive price. For example, the US mines near <a href="/wiki/Kings_Mountain,_North_Carolina" title="Kings Mountain, North Carolina">Kings Mountain, North Carolina</a> closed before the beginning of the 21st century.
</p><p>The development of lithium ion batteries increased the demand for lithium and became the dominant use in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSYB1994_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSYB1994-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> With the surge of lithium demand in batteries in the 2000s, new companies have expanded brine extraction efforts to meet the rising demand.<sup id="cite_ref-IMR_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMR-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_lithium_production" title="List of countries by lithium production">List of countries by lithium production</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:273px;max-width:273px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Lithium_mine,_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto,_Argentina.jpg" class="image"><img alt="alt1" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg/160px-Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg" width="160" height="107" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg/240px-Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg/320px-Lithium_mine%2C_Salar_del_Hombre_Muerto%2C_Argentina.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:107px;max-width:107px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Uyuni_landsat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="alt2" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Uyuni_landsat.JPG/105px-Uyuni_landsat.JPG" width="105" height="105" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Uyuni_landsat.JPG/158px-Uyuni_landsat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Uyuni_landsat.JPG/210px-Uyuni_landsat.JPG 2x" data-file-width="526" data-file-height="526" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Satellite images of the Salar del Hombre Muerto, Argentina (left), and <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Uyuni</a>, Bolivia (right), <a href="/wiki/Salt_pan_(geology)" title="Salt pan (geology)">salt flats</a> that are rich in lithium. The lithium-rich brine is concentrated by pumping it into <a href="/wiki/Salt_evaporation_pond" title="Salt evaporation pond">solar evaporation ponds</a> (visible in the left image).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lithium_world_production.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Lithium_world_production.svg/220px-Lithium_world_production.svg.png" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Lithium_world_production.svg/330px-Lithium_world_production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Lithium_world_production.svg/440px-Lithium_world_production.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lithium_world_production.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production trend of lithium</div></div></div>
<p>Lithium production has greatly increased since the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The metal is separated from other elements in <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous minerals</a>. The metal is produced through <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> from a mixture of fused 55% <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">lithium chloride</a> and 45% <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> at about 450&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2015, most of the world's lithium production is in South America, where lithium-containing brine is extracted from underground pools and concentrated by solar evaporation.  The standard extraction technique is to evaporate water from brine. Each batch takes from 18 to 24 months.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, the price of lithium was about <span class="nowrap">95 USD/kg</span> (or 43 USD/<a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ober_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ober-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reserves">Reserves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reserves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Worldwide identified reserves in 2018 are estimated by the <a href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="US Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a> (USGS) to be 16 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-minerals.usgs.gov_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minerals.usgs.gov-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> though an accurate estimate of world lithium reserves is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-gold_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gold-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forbes_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> One reason for this is that most lithium classification schemes are developed for solid ore deposits, whereas brine is a fluid that is problematic to treat with the same classification scheme due to varying concentrations and pumping effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston2011_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston2011-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The world has been estimated to contain about 15 million tonnes of lithium reserves, while 65 million tonnes of known resources are reasonable. A total of 75% of everything can typically be found in the ten largest deposits of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Vikstrom_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vikstrom-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Another study noted that 83% of the geological resources of lithium are located in six brine, two pegmatite, and two sedimentary deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-Grosjean2011_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grosjean2011-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The world’s top 3 lithium-producing countries from 2016, as reported by the US Geological Survey are Australia, Chile and Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The intersection of <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> make up the region known as the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium_Triangle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lithium Triangle (page does not exist)">Lithium Triangle</a>. The Lithium Triangle is known for its high quality salt flats including Bolivia's <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Salar de Uyuni</a>, Chile's <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Atacama" title="Salar de Atacama">Salar de Atacama</a>, and Argentina's <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Arizaro" title="Salar de Arizaro">Salar de Arizaro</a>. The Lithium Triangle is believed to contain over 75% of existing known lithium reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Deposits are found in South America throughout the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> mountain chain. Chile is the leading producer, followed by Argentina. Both countries recover lithium from brine pools.  According to USGS, Bolivia's <a href="/wiki/Uyuni" title="Uyuni">Uyuni</a> Desert has 5.4 million tonnes of lithium.<sup id="cite_ref-romero_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-romero-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Half the world's known reserves are located in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> along the central eastern slope of the Andes. In 2009, Bolivia negotiated with Japanese, French, and Korean firms to begin extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-romero_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-romero-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
