<p>In the US, lithium is recovered from brine pools in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A deposit discovered in 2013 in Wyoming's <a href="/wiki/Rock_Springs_Uplift" title="Rock Springs Uplift">Rock Springs Uplift</a> is estimated to contain 228,000 tons. Additional deposits in the same formation were estimated to be as much as 18 million tons.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Opinions differ about potential growth. A 2008 study concluded that "realistically achievable lithium carbonate production will be sufficient for only a small fraction of future <a href="/wiki/PHEV" class="mw-redirect" title="PHEV">PHEV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">EV</a> global market requirements", that "demand from the portable electronics sector will absorb much of the planned production increases in the next decade", and that "mass production of lithium carbonate is not environmentally sound, it will cause irreparable ecological damage to ecosystems that should be protected and that <a href="/wiki/LiIon" class="mw-redirect" title="LiIon">LiIon</a> propulsion is incompatible with the notion of the 'Green Car'".<sup id="cite_ref-meridian_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meridian-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2011 study by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Berkeley_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory">Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, the currently estimated reserve base of lithium should not be a limiting factor for large-scale battery production for electric vehicles because an estimated 1 billion 40 <a href="/wiki/KWh" class="mw-redirect" title="KWh">kWh</a> Li-based batteries could be built with current reserves<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> - about 10&#160;kg of lithium per car.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Another 2011 study at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> found enough resources to support global demand until 2100, including the lithium required for the potential widespread transportation use. The study estimated global reserves at 39 million tons, and total demand for lithium during the 90-year period annualized at 12–20 million tons, depending on the scenarios regarding economic growth and recycling rates.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 9, 2014, the <i>Financialist</i> stated that demand for lithium was growing at more than 12% a year. According to Credit Suisse, this rate exceeds projected availability by 25%. The publication compared the 2014 lithium situation with oil, whereby "higher oil prices spurred investment in expensive deepwater and oil sands production techniques"; that is, the price of lithium will continue to rise until more expensive production methods that can boost total output receive the attention of investors.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 16, 2018 2.5 million tonnes of high-grade lithium resources and 124 million pounds of uranium resources were found in the Falchani hard rock deposit in the region Puno, Peru.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pricing">Pricing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pricing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">2007 financial crisis</a>, major suppliers, such as <a href="/wiki/Sociedad_Qu%C3%ADmica_y_Minera" title="Sociedad Química y Minera">Sociedad Química y Minera</a> (SQM), dropped <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">lithium carbonate</a> pricing by 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Prices rose in 2012. A 2012 <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Business Week</a> article outlined the <a href="/wiki/Oligopoly" title="Oligopoly">oligopoly</a> in the lithium space: "SQM, controlled by billionaire <a href="/wiki/Julio_Ponce_Lerou" title="Julio Ponce Lerou">Julio Ponce</a>, is the second-largest, followed by <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_Corporation#Acquisition_of_Rockwood_Holdings" title="Albemarle Corporation">Rockwood</a>, which is backed by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kravis" title="Henry Kravis">Henry Kravis’s</a> KKR &amp; Co., and Philadelphia-based FMC", with <a href="/wiki/Talison_Minerals" title="Talison Minerals">Talison</a> mentioned as the biggest producer.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Global consumption may jump to 300,000 metric tons a year by 2020 from about 150,000 tons in 2012, to match the demand for lithium batteries that has been growing at about 25% a year, outpacing the 4% to 5% overall gain in lithium production.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraction">Extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium salts are extracted from water in <a href="/wiki/Mineral_springs" class="mw-redirect" title="Mineral springs">mineral springs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> pools, and brine deposits. Brine excavation is probably the only lithium extraction technology widely used today, as actual mining of lithium ores is much more expensive and has been priced out of the market.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium is present in seawater, but commercially viable methods of extraction have yet to be developed.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another potential source of lithium is the leachates of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Geothermal electricity">geothermal wells</a>, which are carried to the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-bourcier_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourcier-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Recovery of lithium has been demonstrated in the field; the lithium is separated by simple filtration.<sup id="cite_ref-Simbol_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simbol-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The process and environmental costs are primarily those of the already-operating well; net environmental impacts may thus be positive.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment">Investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Investment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/50px-Mergefrom.svg.png" width="50" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/75px-Mergefrom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/100px-Mergefrom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="50" data-file-height="20" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b><i><a href="/wiki/Lithium_as_an_investment" title="Lithium as an investment">Lithium as an investment</a></i> is in the process of being <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Merging" title="Wikipedia:Merging">merged</a> into this <a href="/wiki/Lithium#Investment" title="Lithium">section</a></b>. <br /> If possible, please edit only this article, as the article mentioned above may be turned into a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Redirect" title="Wikipedia:Redirect">redirect</a>. Relevant discussion may be found on <a href="/wiki/Talk:Lithium" title="Talk:Lithium">this article's talk page</a>&#32;and/or <a href="/wiki/Talk:Lithium_as_an_investment" title="Talk:Lithium as an investment">the source article's talk page</a>.  <small><i>(June 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Currently, there are a number of options available in the marketplace to invest in the metal. While buying physical stock of lithium is hardly possible, investors can buy shares of companies engaged in lithium mining and producing.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Also, investors can purchase a dedicated lithium ETF offering exposure to a group of commodity producers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Lithium_Uses.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Global_Lithium_Uses.svg/220px-Global_Lithium_Uses.svg.png" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Global_Lithium_Uses.svg/330px-Global_Lithium_Uses.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Global_Lithium_Uses.svg/440px-Global_Lithium_Uses.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="199" data-file-height="188" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Lithium_Uses.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimates of global lithium uses in 2011 (picture) and 2015 (numbers below)<sup id="cite_ref-Li-uses-2011_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li-uses-2011-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef4f30; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Ceramics and glass (32%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f9af20; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Batteries (35%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f2eb23; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Lubricating greases (9%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a5e429; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Continuous casting (5%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00e8cf; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Air treatment (5%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #103dc9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Polymers (4%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a53ae0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Primary aluminum production (1%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e200f4; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Pharmaceuticals (&lt;1%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #da003e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Other (9%)</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceramics_and_glass">Ceramics and glass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ceramics and glass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium oxide is widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Flux_(metallurgy)" title="Flux (metallurgy)">flux</a> for processing <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a>, reducing the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of the material and leading to <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glazes</a> with improved physical properties including low coefficients of thermal expansion. Worldwide, this is one of the largest use for lithium compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Li-uses-2011_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li-uses-2011-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Glazes containing lithium oxides are used for ovenware. <a href="/wiki/Lithium_carbonate" title="Lithium carbonate">Lithium carbonate</a> (Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) is generally used in this application  because it converts to the oxide upon heating.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_and_electronics">Electrical and electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Electrical and electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Late in the 20th century, lithium became an important component of battery electrolytes and electrodes, because of its high <a href="/wiki/Electrode_potential" title="Electrode potential">electrode potential</a>. Because of its low <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a>, it has a high charge- and power-to-weight ratio. A typical <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a> can generate approximately 3 <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volts</a> per cell, compared with 2.1 volts for <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead-acid</a> and 1.5 volts for <a href="/wiki/Zinc-carbon_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc-carbon cell">zinc-carbon</a>. Lithium-ion batteries, which are rechargeable and have a high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a>, differ from <a href="/wiki/Lithium_batteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium batteries">lithium batteries</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Disposable" class="mw-redirect" title="Disposable">disposable</a> (<a href="/wiki/Primary_cell" title="Primary cell">primary</a>) <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> with lithium or its compounds as the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Other rechargeable batteries that use lithium include the <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_polymer_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium-ion polymer battery">lithium-ion polymer battery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iron_phosphate_battery" title="Lithium iron phosphate battery">lithium iron phosphate battery</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nanowire_battery" title="Nanowire battery">nanowire battery</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lubricating_greases">Lubricating greases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lubricating greases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lithium_grease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium grease">Lithium grease</a></div>
<p>The third most common use of lithium is in greases. Lithium hydroxide is a strong <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> and, when heated with a fat, produces a soap made of lithium <a href="/wiki/Stearate" title="Stearate">stearate</a>. Lithium soap has the ability to <a href="/wiki/Thickening_agent" title="Thickening agent">thicken</a> oils, and it is used to manufacture all-purpose, high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Grease_(lubricant)" title="Grease (lubricant)">lubricating greases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Metallurgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium (e.g. as lithium carbonate) is used as an additive to <a href="/wiki/Continuous_casting" title="Continuous casting">continuous casting</a> mould flux slags where it increases fluidity,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> a use which accounts for 5% of global lithium use (2011).<sup id="cite_ref-minerals.usgs.gov_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minerals.usgs.gov-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Lithium compounds are also used as additives (fluxes) to <a href="/wiki/Foundry_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Foundry sand">foundry sand</a> for iron casting to reduce veining.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium (as  <a href="/wiki/Lithium_fluoride" title="Lithium fluoride">lithium fluoride</a>) is used as an additive to aluminium smelters (<a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>), reducing melting temperature and increasing electrical resistance,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> a use which accounts for 3% of production (2011).<sup id="cite_ref-minerals.usgs.gov_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minerals.usgs.gov-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When used as a <a href="/wiki/Flux_(metallurgy)" title="Flux (metallurgy)">flux</a> for <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> or <a href="/wiki/Soldering" title="Soldering">soldering</a>, metallic lithium promotes the fusing of metals during the process<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and eliminates the forming of <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> by absorbing impurities.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">Alloys</a> of the metal with aluminium, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, copper and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> are used to make high-performance aircraft parts (see also <a href="/wiki/Al-Li" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Li">Lithium-aluminium alloys</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon_nano-welding">Silicon nano-welding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Silicon nano-welding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium has been found effective in assisting the perfection of silicon nano-welds in electronic components for electric batteries and other devices.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_chemical_and_industrial_uses">Other chemical and industrial uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other chemical and industrial uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png/110px-Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png" width="110" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png/165px-Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png/220px-Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png 2x" data-file-width="495" data-file-height="1401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flammenf%C3%A4rbungLi.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lithium use in flares and <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a> is due to its rose-red flame.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pyrotechnics">Pyrotechnics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pyrotechnics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Lithium compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic_colorant" title="Pyrotechnic colorant">pyrotechnic colorants</a> and oxidizers in red <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flare_(pyrotechnic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flare (pyrotechnic)">flares</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_purification">Air purification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Air purification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">Lithium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_bromide" title="Lithium bromide">lithium bromide</a> are <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> and are used as <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccants</a> for gas streams.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  Lithium hydroxide and <a href="/wiki/Lithium_peroxide" title="Lithium peroxide">lithium peroxide</a> are the salts most used in confined areas, such as aboard <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>, for carbon dioxide removal and air purification. Lithium hydroxide absorbs <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the air by forming lithium carbonate, and is preferred over other alkaline hydroxides for its low weight.
</p><p>Lithium peroxide (Li<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) in presence of moisture not only reacts with carbon dioxide to form lithium carbonate, but also releases oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The reaction is as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Li<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> + 2 CO<sub>2</sub> → 2 Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + O<sub>2</sub>.</dd></dl>
<p>Some of the aforementioned compounds, as well as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_perchlorate" title="Lithium perchlorate">lithium perchlorate</a>, are used in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator#Oxygen_candle" title="Chemical oxygen generator">oxygen candles</a> that supply <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. These can also include small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminum" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_fluoride" title="Lithium fluoride">Lithium fluoride</a>, artificially grown as <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a>, is clear and transparent and often used in specialist optics for <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> and VUV (<a href="/wiki/Vacuum_UV" class="mw-redirect" title="Vacuum UV">vacuum UV</a>) applications. It has one of the lowest <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive indexes</a> and the furthest transmission range in the deep UV of most common materials.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Finely divided lithium fluoride powder has been used for <a href="/wiki/Thermoluminescent_Dosimeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermoluminescent Dosimeter">thermoluminescent radiation dosimetry</a> (TLD): when a sample of such is exposed to radiation, it accumulates <a href="/wiki/Crystal_defect" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystal defect">crystal defects</a> which, when heated, resolve via a release of bluish light whose intensity is proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Absorbed_dose" title="Absorbed dose">absorbed dose</a>, thus allowing this to be quantified.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Lithium fluoride is sometimes used in focal lenses of <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The high non-linearity of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_niobate" title="Lithium niobate">lithium niobate</a> also makes it useful in <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_optics" title="Nonlinear optics">non-linear optics applications</a>. It is used extensively in telecommunication products such as mobile phones and <a href="/wiki/Optical_modulator" title="Optical modulator">optical modulators</a>, for such components as <a href="/wiki/Crystal_oscillator" title="Crystal oscillator">resonant crystals</a>. Lithium applications are used in more than 60% of mobile phones.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_and_polymer_chemistry">Organic and polymer chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Organic and polymer chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organolithium compound">Organolithium compounds</a> are widely used in the production of polymer and fine-chemicals. In the polymer industry, which is the dominant consumer of these reagents, alkyl lithium compounds are <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a>/<a href="/wiki/Radical_initiator" title="Radical initiator">initiators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Anionic_addition_polymerization" title="Anionic addition polymerization">anionic polymerization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">unfunctionalized</a> <a href="/wiki/Olefin" class="mw-redirect" title="Olefin">olefins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> For the production of fine chemicals, organolithium compounds function as strong bases and as reagents for the formation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-carbon_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon-carbon bond">carbon-carbon bonds</a>. Organolithium compounds are prepared from lithium metal and alkyl halides.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many other lithium compounds are used as reagents to prepare organic compounds. Some popular compounds include <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a> (LiAlH<sub>4</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Lithium_triethylborohydride" title="Lithium triethylborohydride">lithium triethylborohydride</a>, <a href="/wiki/N-butyllithium" class="mw-redirect" title="N-butyllithium">n-butyllithium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tert-butyllithium" class="mw-redirect" title="Tert-butyllithium">tert-butyllithium</a> are commonly used as extremely strong bases called <a href="/wiki/Superbase" title="Superbase">superbases</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_applications">Military applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Military applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Metallic lithium and its complex <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydrides</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">Li[AlH<sub>4</sub>]</a>, are used as high-energy additives to <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">rocket propellants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-emsley_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Lithium aluminum hydride can also be used by itself as a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_(ASW)_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_(DDG_84).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg/220px-US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg/330px-US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg/440px-US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_%28ASW%29_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_%28DDG_84%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="1960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_040626-N-5319A-006_An_Anti-Submarine_Warfare_(ASW)_MK-50_Torpedo_is_launched_from_guided_missile_destroyer_USS_Bulkeley_(DDG_84).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The launch of a torpedo using lithium as fuel</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mark_50_torpedo" title="Mark 50 torpedo">Mark 50 torpedo</a> stored chemical energy propulsion system (SCEPS) uses a small tank of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> gas, which is sprayed over a block of solid lithium. The reaction generates heat, creating <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> to propel the torpedo in a closed <a href="/wiki/Rankine_cycle" title="Rankine cycle">Rankine cycle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">Lithium hydride</a> containing lithium-6 is used in <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">thermonuclear weapons</a>, where it serves as fuel for the fusion stage of the bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear">Nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium-6 is valued as a source material for <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> production and as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_absorber" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron absorber">neutron absorber</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. Natural lithium contains about 7.5% lithium-6 from which large amounts of lithium-6 have been produced by <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">isotope separation</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Lithium-7 gained interest for use in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a> <a href="/wiki/Coolant" title="Coolant">coolants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg/220px-Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg/330px-Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg/440px-Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Bravo_Blast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lithium deuteride was used as fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bravo" title="Castle Bravo">Castle Bravo</a> nuclear device.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_deuteride" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium deuteride">Lithium deuteride</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion fuel</a> of choice in early versions of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">hydrogen bomb</a>. When bombarded by <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, both <sup>6</sup>Li and <sup>7</sup>Li produce <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> — this reaction, which was not fully understood when <a href="/wiki/Teller-Ulam_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Teller-Ulam design">hydrogen bombs</a> were first tested, was responsible for the runaway yield of the <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bravo" title="Castle Bravo">Castle Bravo</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear test">nuclear test</a>. Tritium fuses with <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> reaction that is relatively easy to achieve. Although details remain secret, lithium-6 deuteride apparently still plays a role in modern <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> as a fusion material.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lithium_fluoride" title="Lithium fluoride">Lithium fluoride</a>, when highly enriched in the lithium-7 isotope, forms the basic constituent of the fluoride salt mixture LiF-<a href="/wiki/Beryllium_fluoride" title="Beryllium fluoride">BeF<sub>2</sub></a> used in <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">liquid fluoride nuclear reactors</a>. Lithium fluoride is exceptionally chemically stable and LiF-BeF<sub>2</sub> mixtures have low melting points. In addition, <sup>7</sup>Li, Be, and F are among the few <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a> with low enough <a href="/wiki/Neutron_cross-section" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron cross-section">thermal neutron capture cross-sections</a> not to poison the fission reactions inside a nuclear fission reactor.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In conceptualized (hypothetical) nuclear <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">fusion power</a> plants, lithium will be used to produce tritium in <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_confinement_fusion" title="Magnetic confinement fusion">magnetically confined reactors</a> using <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> as the fuel. Naturally occurring tritium is extremely rare, and must be synthetically produced by surrounding the reacting <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> with a 'blanket' containing lithium where neutrons from the deuterium-tritium reaction in the plasma will fission the lithium to produce more tritium:
</p>
<dl><dd><sup>6</sup>Li + n → <sup>4</sup>He + <sup>3</sup>H.</dd></dl>
<p>Lithium is also used as a source for <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> nuclei. When <sup>7</sup>Li is bombarded by accelerated <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> <sup>8</sup><a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">Be</a> is formed, which undergoes fission to form two alpha particles. This feat, called "splitting the atom" at the time, was the first fully man-made <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a>. It was produced by <a href="/wiki/John_Douglas_Cockcroft" class="mw-redirect" title="John Douglas Cockcroft">Cockroft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Walton" title="Ernest Walton">Walton</a> in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, the US <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> said a shortage of lithium-7 critical to the operation of 65 out of 100 American nuclear reactors “places their ability to continue to provide electricity at some risk”. The problem stems from the decline of US nuclear infrastructure. The equipment needed to separate lithium-6 from lithium-7 is mostly a cold war leftover. The US shut down most of this machinery in 1963, when it had a huge surplus of separated lithium, mostly consumed during the twentieth century. The report said it would take five years and $10 million to $12 million to reestablish the ability to separate lithium-6 from lithium-7.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1013_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1013-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reactors that use lithium-7 heat water under high pressure and transfer heat through heat exchangers that are prone to corrosion. The reactors use lithium to counteract the corrosive effects of <a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">boric acid</a>, which is added to the water to absorb excess neutrons.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1013_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1013-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lithium_(medication)" title="Lithium (medication)">Lithium (medication)</a></div>
<p>Lithium is useful in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kean_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kean-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Lithium salts may also be helpful for related diagnoses, such as <a href="/wiki/Schizoaffective_disorder" title="Schizoaffective disorder">schizoaffective disorder</a> and cyclic <a href="/wiki/Major_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Major depression">major depression</a>. The active part of these salts is the lithium ion Li<sup>+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-kean_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kean-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> They may increase the risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Ebstein%27s_anomaly" title="Ebstein&#39;s anomaly">Ebstein's cardiac anomaly</a> in infants born to women who take lithium during the first trimester of pregnancy.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid18982835_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18982835-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lithium has also been researched as a possible treatment for <a href="/wiki/Cluster_headache" title="Cluster headache">cluster headaches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lithium_(medication)" title="Lithium (medication)">Lithium (medication)</a></div>
<p>Primary food sources of lithium are grains and vegetables, and, in some areas, <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> also contains significant amounts.<sup id="cite_ref-schrauzer_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schrauzer-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Human intake varies depending on location and diet.
</p><p>Lithium was first detected in human organs and fetal tissues in the late 19th century. In humans there are no defined lithium deficiency diseases, but low lithium intakes from water supplies were associated with increased rates of suicides, homicides and the arrest rates for drug use and other crimes. The biochemical mechanisms of action of lithium appear to be multifactorial and are intercorrelated with the functions of several enzymes, hormones and vitamins, as well as with growth and transforming factors. Evidence now appears to be sufficient to accept lithium as essential; a provisional RDA of 1,000&#160;µg/day is suggested for a 70&#160;kg adult.<sup id="cite_ref-schrauzer_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schrauzer-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</td>

<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Globally_Harmonized_System_of_Classification_and_Labelling_of_Chemicals" title="Globally Harmonized System of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals">GHS signal word</a>
</td>
<td>Danger
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/GHS_hazard_statement" class="mw-redirect" title="GHS hazard statement">GHS hazard statements</a></div>
</td>
<td><abbr class="abbr" title="In contact with water releases flammable gases which may ignite spontaneously">H260</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Causes severe skin burns and eye damage">H314</abbr>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/GHS_precautionary_statements" title="GHS precautionary statements">GHS precautionary statements</a></div>

<td><abbr class="abbr" title="Keep away from any possible contact with water, because of violent reaction and possible flash fire">P223</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Handle under inert gas. Protect from moisture">P231+232</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="Wear protective gloves/protective clothing/eye protection/face protection">P280</abbr>, <abbr class="abbr" title="IF IN EYES: Rinse continuously with water for several minutes. <tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="refs" style="float:right; padding:1px; text-align:right;"><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></div><div id="container" style="float:left; margin-left:1em; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div id="on_image_elements" class="nounderlines" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_66973216"><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" coords="40,0,60,20,40,40,20,20" alt="Flammability code 2: Must be moderately heated or exposed to relatively high ambient temperature before ignition can occur. Flash point between 38 and 93 &#xb0;C (100 and 200 &#xb0;F). E.g., diesel fuel" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" shape="poly" coords="20,20,40,40,20,60,0,40" alt="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas" title="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" shape="poly" coords="60,20,80,40,60,60,40,40" alt="Reactivity code 2: Undergoes violent chemical change at elevated temperatures and pressures, reacts violently with water, or may form explosive mixtures with water. E.g., phosphorus" title="Reactivity code 2: Undergoes violent chemical change at elevated temperatures and pressures, reacts violently with water, or may form explosive mixtures with water. E.g., phosphorus" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" alt="Special hazard W: Reacts with water in an unusual or dangerous manner. E.g., cesium, sodium" title="Special hazard W: Reacts with water in an unusual or dangerous manner. E.g., cesium, sodium" /></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" width="80" height="80" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="600" usemap="#ImageMap_1_66973216" /></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 2: Must be moderately heated or exposed to relatively high ambient temperature before ignition can occur. Flash point between 38 and 93 °C (100 and 200 °F). E.g., diesel fuel">2</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 3: Short exposure could cause serious temporary or residual injury. E.g., chlorine gas">3</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 2: Undergoes violent chemical change at elevated temperatures and pressures, reacts violently with water, or may form explosive mixtures with water. E.g., phosphorus">2</span></a></div><div style="vertical-align:middle; text-align:center; line-height:80%; position:absolute; top:52px;"><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" title="NFPA 704"><span style="font-size:15px; color:black; width:23px; position:absolute; left:29px;" title="Special hazard W: Reacts with water in an unusual or dangerous manner. E.g., cesium, sodium"><s>W</s></span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>Lithium is <a href="/wiki/Corrosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Corrosive">corrosive</a> and requires special handling to avoid skin contact. Breathing lithium dust or lithium compounds (which are often alkaline) initially <a href="/wiki/Irritation" title="Irritation">irritate</a> the <a href="/wiki/Human_nose" title="Human nose">nose</a> and throat, while higher exposure can cause a buildup of fluid in the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, leading to <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a>. The metal itself is a handling hazard because contact with moisture produces the <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">caustic</a> lithium hydroxide. Lithium is safely stored in non-reactive compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some jurisdictions limit the sale of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_batteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium batteries">lithium batteries</a>, which are the most readily available source of lithium for ordinary consumers. Lithium can be used to reduce <a href="/wiki/Pseudoephedrine" title="Pseudoephedrine">pseudoephedrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ephedrine" title="Ephedrine">ephedrine</a> to <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Birch_reduction" title="Birch reduction">Birch reduction</a> method, which employs solutions of alkali metals dissolved in anhydrous <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Carriage and shipment of some kinds of lithium batteries may be prohibited aboard certain types of transportation (particularly aircraft) because of the ability of most types of lithium batteries to fully discharge very rapidly when <a href="/wiki/Short_circuit" title="Short circuit">short-circuited</a>, leading to overheating and possible <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosion</a> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">thermal runaway</a>. Most consumer lithium batteries have built-in thermal overload protection to prevent this type of incident, or are otherwise designed to limit short-circuit currents. Internal shorts from manufacturing defect or physical damage can lead to spontaneous thermal runaway.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lithium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-res-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-res_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-res_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://minerals.usgs.gov/minerals/pubs/mcs/2011/mcsapp2011.pdf">Appendixes</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111106013449/http://minerals.usgs.gov/minerals/pubs/mcs/2011/mcsapp2011.pdf">Archived</a> 6 November 2011 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.. By USGS definitions, the reserve base "may encompass those parts of the resources that have a reasonable potential for becoming economically available within planning horizons beyond those that assume proven technology and current economics. The reserve base includes those resources that are currently economic (reserves), marginally economic (marginal reserves), and some of those that are currently subeconomic (subeconomic resources)."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2010</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2013</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Beryllium and fluorine occur only as one isotope, <sup>9</sup>Be and <sup>19</sup>F respectively. These two, together with <sup>7</sup>Li, as well as <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium"><sup>2</sup>H</a>, <sup>11</sup>B, <sup>15</sup>N, <sup>209</sup>Bi, and the stable isotopes of C, and O, are the only nuclides with low enough thermal neutron capture cross sections aside from <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> to serve as major constituents of a molten salt breeder reactor fuel.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Berzelius (1817). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kAsAAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA44">"Ein neues mineralisches Alkali und ein neues Metall"</a> &#91;A new mineral alkali and a new metal&#93;. <i>Journal für Chemie und Physik</i>. <b>21</b>: 44–48. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161203044634/https://books.google.com/books?id=kAsAAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA44">Archived</a> from the original on 3 December 2016.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+f%C3%BCr+Chemie+und+Physik&amp;rft.atitle=Ein+neues+mineralisches+Alkali+und+ein+neues+Metall&amp;rft.volume=21&amp;rft.pages=44-48&amp;rft.date=1817&amp;rft.au=Berzelius&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DkAsAAAAAMAAJ%26pg%3DPA44&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALithium" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/>  From p. 45:  <i>"Herr </i>August Arfwedson<i>, ein junger sehr verdienstvoller Chemiker, der seit einem Jahre in meinem Laboratorie arbeitet, fand bei einer Analyse des Petalits von Uto's Eisengrube, einen alkalischen Bestandtheil, …  Wir haben es </i>Lithion<i> genannt, um dadurch auf seine erste Entdeckung im Mineralreich anzuspielen, da die beiden anderen erst in der organischen Natur entdeckt wurden.  Sein Radical wird dann Lithium genannt werden."</i> (Mr. <i>August Arfwedson</i>, a young, very meritorious chemist, who has worked in my laboratory for a year, found during an analysis of petalite from Uto's iron mine, an alkaline component …  We've named it <i>lithion</i>, in order to allude thereby to its first discovery in the mineral realm, since the two others were first discovered in organic nature.  Its radical will then be named "lithium".)</span>
